scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">House</a></th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">Stuart</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Father</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I of England</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Mother</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Maria_of_France" title="Henrietta Maria of France">Henrietta Maria of France</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Signature</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/File:CharlesIISig.svg" class="image" title="Charles II&#39;s signature"><img alt="Charles II&#39;s signature" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/CharlesIISig.svg/125px-CharlesIISig.svg.png" width="125" height="52" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/CharlesIISig.svg/188px-CharlesIISig.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/CharlesIISig.svg/250px-CharlesIISig.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="462" data-file-height="194" /></a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Charles II</b> (29 May 1630&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 6 February 1685)<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> was king of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>. He was king of Scotland from 1649 until his deposition in 1651, and king of England, Scotland and Ireland from the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(1660)" title="Restoration (1660)">restoration of the monarchy</a> in 1660 until his death. \n</p><p>Charles II\'s father, <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England#Execution" title="Charles I of England">was executed</a> at <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Whitehall" title="Palace of Whitehall">Whitehall</a> on 30 January 1649, at the climax of the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. Although the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> proclaimed Charles II king on 5 February 1649, England entered the period known as the <a href="/wiki/Interregnum_(England)" title="Interregnum (England)">English Interregnum</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">English Commonwealth</a>, and the country was a <i>de facto</i> republic, led by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>. Cromwell defeated Charles II at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Worcester" title="Battle of Worcester">Battle of Worcester</a> on 3 September 1651, and Charles <a href="/wiki/Escape_of_Charles_II" title="Escape of Charles II">fled to mainland Europe</a>. Cromwell became virtual dictator of England, Scotland and Ireland. Charles spent the next nine years in exile in France, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Netherlands" title="Spanish Netherlands">Spanish Netherlands</a>. A political crisis that followed the death of Cromwell in 1658 resulted in the <a href="/wiki/English_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="English Restoration">restoration</a> of the monarchy, and Charles was invited to return to Britain. On 29 May 1660, his 30th birthday, he was received in London to public acclaim. After 1660, all legal documents were dated as if he had succeeded his father as king in 1649.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cavalier_Parliament" title="Cavalier Parliament">Charles\'s English parliament</a> enacted laws known as the <a href="/wiki/Clarendon_Code" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarendon Code">Clarendon Code</a>, designed to shore up the position of the re-<a href="/wiki/Established_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established Church">established</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. Charles acquiesced to the Clarendon Code even though he favoured a policy of religious tolerance. The major foreign policy issue of his early reign was the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Dutch_War" title="Second Anglo-Dutch War">Second Anglo-Dutch War</a>. In 1670, he entered into the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Dover" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Dover">Treaty of Dover</a>, an alliance with his first cousin King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV of France</a>. Louis agreed to aid him in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Dutch_War" title="Third Anglo-Dutch War">Third Anglo-Dutch War</a> and pay him a pension, and Charles secretly promised to convert to Catholicism at an unspecified future date. Charles attempted to introduce <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">religious freedom</a> for Catholics and Protestant <a href="/wiki/Dissenter" title="Dissenter">dissenters</a> with his 1672 <a href="/wiki/Royal_Declaration_of_Indulgence" title="Royal Declaration of Indulgence">Royal Declaration of Indulgence</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/English_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="English Parliament">English Parliament</a> forced him to withdraw it. In 1679, <a href="/wiki/Titus_Oates" title="Titus Oates">Titus Oates</a>\'s revelations of a supposed "<a href="/wiki/Popish_Plot" title="Popish Plot">Popish Plot</a>" sparked the <a href="/wiki/Exclusion_Crisis" title="Exclusion Crisis">Exclusion Crisis</a> when it was revealed that Charles\'s brother and heir (<a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James, Duke of York</a>) was a Catholic. The crisis saw the birth of the pro-exclusion <a href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Whig Party">Whig</a> and anti-exclusion <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tory</a> parties. Charles sided with the Tories, and, following the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Rye_House_Plot" title="Rye House Plot">Rye House Plot</a> to murder Charles and James in 1683, some Whig leaders were executed or forced into exile. Charles dissolved the English Parliament in 1681, and ruled alone until his death on 6 February 1685. He was received into the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> on his deathbed.\n</p><p>Charles was one of the most popular and beloved kings of England,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgg1955139_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgg1955139-4">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> known as the <i>Merry Monarch</i>, in reference to both the liveliness and hedonism of his court and the general relief at the return to normality after over a decade of rule by Cromwell and the <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a>. Charles\'s wife, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Braganza" title="Catherine of Braganza">Catherine of Braganza</a>, bore no live children, but Charles acknowledged at least twelve illegitimate children by various mistresses. He was succeeded by his brother James.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div with Parliament</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Later_years"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Later years</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Titles,_styles,_honours_and_arms"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Titles, styles, honours and arms</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Titles_and_styles"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Titles and styles</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Honours"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Honours</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Arms"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Arms</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Issue"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Issue</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Ancestry"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Ancestry</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span id="Early_life.2C_civil_war_and_exile"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life,_civil_war_and_exile">Early life, civil war and exile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life, civil war and exile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_II_Prince_of_Wales_Egmont.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Baby in white christening robe" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Charles_II_Prince_of_Wales_Egmont.jpg/170px-Charles_II_Prince_of_Wales_Egmont.jpg" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Charles_II_Prince_of_Wales_Egmont.jpg/255px-Charles_II_Prince_of_Wales_Egmont.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Charles_II_Prince_of_Wales_Egmont.jpg/340px-Charles_II_Prince_of_Wales_Egmont.jpg 2x" data-file-width="770" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_II_Prince_of_Wales_Egmont.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles II as an infant in 1630, painting attributed to <a href="/wiki/Justus_van_Egmont" title="Justus van Egmont">Justus van Egmont</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Dobson_-_Charles_II,_1630_-_1685._King_of_Scots_1649_-_1685._King_of_England_and_Ireland_1660_-_1685_(When_Prince_of_Wales,_with_a_page)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Charles as a boy with shoulder-length black hair and standing in a martial pose" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/William_Dobson_-_Charles_II%2C_1630_-_1685._King_of_Scots_1649_-_1685._King_of_England_and_Ireland_1660_-_1685_%28When_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_a_page%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/William_Dobson_-_Charles_II%2C_1630_-_1685._King_of_Scots_1649_-_1685._King_of_England_and_Ireland_1660_-_1685_%28When_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_a_page%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/William_Dobson_-_Charles_II%2C_1630_-_1685._King_of_Scots_1649_-_1685._King_of_England_and_Ireland_1660_-_1685_%28When_Prince_of_Wales%2C_with_a_page%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3364" data-file-height="4001" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Dobson_-_Charles_II,_1630_-_1685._King_of_Scots_1649_-_1685._King_of_England_and_Ireland_1660_-_1685_(When_Prince_of_Wales,_with_a_page)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by <a href="/wiki/William_Dobson" title="William Dobson">William Dobson</a>, c. 1642 or 1643</div></div></div>\n<p>Charles II was born at <a href="/wiki/St_James%27s_Palace" title="St James&#39;s Palace">St James\'s Palace</a> on 29 May 1630. His parents were <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> (who ruled the three kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Maria_of_France" title="Henrietta Maria of France">Henrietta Maria</a> (the sister of the French king <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIII_of_France" title="Louis XIII of France">Louis XIII</a>). Charles was their second child. Their first son was born about a year before Charles, but died within a day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996255\xe2\x80\x93257_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996255\xe2\x80\x93257-5">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> England, Scotland, and Ireland were respectively predominantly <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Anglican</a>, <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a>. Charles was baptised in the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapel Royal</a>, on 27 June, by the Anglican <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_London" title="Bishop of London">Bishop of London</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>. He was brought up in the care of the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Edward_Sackville,_4th_Earl_of_Dorset#Family" title="Edward Sackville, 4th Earl of Dorset">Countess of Dorset</a>, though his godparents included his maternal uncle Louis XIII and his maternal grandmother, <a href="/wiki/Marie_de%27_Medici" title="Marie de&#39; Medici">Marie de\' Medici</a>, the Dowager Queen of France, both of whom were Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> At birth, Charles automatically became <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Cornwall" title="Duke of Cornwall">Duke of Cornwall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Rothesay" title="Duke of Rothesay">Duke of Rothesay</a>, along with several other associated titles. At or around his eighth birthday, he was designated <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>, though he was never formally invested.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996255\xe2\x80\x93257_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996255\xe2\x80\x93257-5">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1640s, when Charles was still young, his father fought <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> forces in the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. Charles accompanied his father during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Edgehill" title="Battle of Edgehill">Battle of Edgehill</a> and, at the age of fourteen, participated in the campaigns of 1645, when he was made titular commander of the English forces in the <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> By spring 1646, his father was losing the war, and Charles left England due to fears for his safety. Setting off from <a href="/wiki/Falmouth,_Cornwall" title="Falmouth, Cornwall">Falmouth</a> after staying at <a href="/wiki/Pendennis_Castle" title="Pendennis Castle">Pendennis Castle</a>, he went first to the <a href="/wiki/Isles_of_Scilly" title="Isles of Scilly">Isles of Scilly</a>, then to <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Jersey</a>, and finally to France, where his mother was already living in exile and his first cousin, eight-year-old <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a>, was king.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Charles I surrendered into captivity in May 1646.\n</p><p>In 1648, during the <a href="/wiki/Second_English_Civil_War" title="Second English Civil War">Second English Civil War</a>, Charles moved to <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a>, where his sister <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Princess_Royal_and_Princess_of_Orange" title="Mary, Princess Royal and Princess of Orange">Mary</a> and his brother-in-law <a href="/wiki/William_II,_Prince_of_Orange" title="William II, Prince of Orange">William II, Prince of Orange</a>, seemed more likely to provide substantial aid to the <a href="/wiki/Cavalier" title="Cavalier">royalist</a> cause than his mother\'s French relations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser197955\xe2\x80\x9356_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser197955\xe2\x80\x9356-9">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, the royalist fleet that came under Charles\'s control was not used to any advantage, and did not reach Scotland in time to join up with the royalist <a href="/wiki/Engager" class="mw-redirect" title="Engager">Engager</a> army of the <a href="/wiki/James_Hamilton,_1st_Duke_of_Hamilton" title="James Hamilton, 1st Duke of Hamilton">Duke of Hamilton</a> before it was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Preston_(1648)" title="Battle of Preston (1648)">Battle of Preston</a> by the Parliamentarians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser197957\xe2\x80\x9360_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser197957\xe2\x80\x9360-10">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At The Hague, Charles had a brief affair with <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Walter" title="Lucy Walter">Lucy Walter</a>, who later falsely claimed that they had secretly married.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Her son, <a href="/wiki/James_Scott,_1st_Duke_of_Monmouth" title="James Scott, 1st Duke of Monmouth">James Crofts</a> (afterwards <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Monmouth" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Monmouth">Duke of Monmouth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Buccleuch" title="Duke of Buccleuch">Duke of Buccleuch</a>), was one of Charles\'s many illegitimate children who became prominent in British society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996255\xe2\x80\x93257_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996255\xe2\x80\x93257-5">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite his son\'s diplomatic efforts to save him, King Charles I was beheaded in January 1649, and England became a republic. On 5 February, the <a href="/wiki/Covenanter" title="Covenanter">Covenanter</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> proclaimed Charles II "King of Great Britain, France and Ireland" at the <a href="/wiki/Mercat_Cross,_Edinburgh" title="Mercat Cross, Edinburgh">Mercat Cross, Edinburgh</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERPS1649/1/71_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERPS1649/1/71-12">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> but refused to allow him to enter Scotland unless he accepted the imposition of Presbyterianism throughout Britain and Ireland.\n</p><p>When negotiations with the Scots stalled, Charles authorised <a href="/wiki/James_Graham,_1st_Marquess_of_Montrose" title="James Graham, 1st Marquess of Montrose">General Montrose</a> to land in the <a href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Orkney Islands">Orkney Islands</a> with a small army to threaten the Scots with invasion, in the hope of forcing an agreement more to his liking. Montrose feared that Charles would accept a compromise, and so chose to invade mainland Scotland anyway. He was captured and executed. Charles reluctantly promised that he would abide by the terms of a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Breda_(1650)" title="Treaty of Breda (1650)">treaty agreed between him and the Scots Parliament</a> at <a href="/wiki/Breda" title="Breda">Breda</a>, and support the <a href="/wiki/Solemn_League_and_Covenant" title="Solemn League and Covenant">Solemn League and Covenant</a>, which authorised <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_church_governance" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian church governance">Presbyterian church governance</a> across Britain. Upon his arrival in Scotland on 23 June 1650, he formally agreed to the Covenant; his abandonment of <a href="/wiki/Episcopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Episcopy">Episcopal</a> church governance, although winning him support in Scotland, left him unpopular in England. Charles himself soon came to despise the "villainy" and "hypocrisy" of the Covenanters.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cast_gold_medal_of_Charles_II_Stuart.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Cast_gold_medal_of_Charles_II_Stuart.jpg/220px-Cast_gold_medal_of_Charles_II_Stuart.jpg" width="220" height="105" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Cast_gold_medal_of_Charles_II_Stuart.jpg/330px-Cast_gold_medal_of_Charles_II_Stuart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Cast_gold_medal_of_Charles_II_Stuart.jpg/440px-Cast_gold_medal_of_Charles_II_Stuart.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="239" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cast_gold_medal_of_Charles_II_Stuart.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cast gold coronation medal of Charles II, dated 1651</div></div></div>\n<p>On 3 September 1650, the Covenanters were defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dunbar_(1650)" title="Battle of Dunbar (1650)">Battle of Dunbar</a> by a much smaller force led by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>. The Scots forces were divided into royalist Engagers and Presbyterian Covenanters, who even fought each other. Disillusioned by the Covenanters, in October Charles attempted to escape from them and rode north to join with an Engager force, an event which became known as "the Start", but within two days the Presbyterians had caught up with and recovered him.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the Scots remained Charles\'s best hope of restoration, and he was crowned King of Scotland at <a href="/wiki/Scone_Abbey" title="Scone Abbey">Scone Abbey</a> on 1 January 1651. With Cromwell\'s forces threatening Charles\'s position in Scotland, it was decided to mount an attack on England. With many of the Scots (including <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Campbell,_1st_Marquess_of_Argyll" title="Archibald Campbell, 1st Marquess of Argyll">Lord Argyll</a> and other leading Covenanters) refusing to participate, and with few English royalists joining the force as it moved south into England, the invasion ended in defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Worcester" title="Battle of Worcester">Battle of Worcester</a> on 3 September 1651, after which Charles eluded capture by hiding in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Oak_(tree)" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Oak (tree)">Royal Oak</a> at <a href="/wiki/Boscobel_House" title="Boscobel House">Boscobel House</a>. Through six weeks of narrow escapes <a href="/wiki/Escape_of_Charles_II" title="Escape of Charles II">Charles managed to flee England</a> in disguise, landing in <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a> on 16 October, despite a reward of <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">\xc2\xa3</a>1,000 on his head, risk of death for anyone caught helping him and the difficulty in disguising Charles, who, at over 6&#160;ft (1.8&#160;m), was unusually tall.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_II_(de_Champaigne).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Charles_II_%28de_Champaigne%29.jpg/220px-Charles_II_%28de_Champaigne%29.jpg" width="220" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Charles_II_%28de_Champaigne%29.jpg/330px-Charles_II_%28de_Champaigne%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/Charles_II_%28de_Champaigne%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="599" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_II_(de_Champaigne).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A king in exile: Charles II painted by <a href="/wiki/Philippe_de_Champaigne" title="Philippe de Champaigne">Philippe de Champaigne</a>, c. 1653</div></div></div>\n<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Instrument_of_Government" title="Instrument of Government">Instrument of Government</a> passed by Parliament, Cromwell was appointed <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector#Cromwellian_Commonwealth" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a> of England, Scotland and Ireland in 1653, effectively placing the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> under military rule. Impoverished, Charles could not obtain sufficient support to mount a serious challenge to Cromwell\'s government. Despite the <a href="/wiki/Stuart_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Stuart family">Stuart family</a> connections through Henrietta Maria and the Princess of Orange, France and the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> allied themselves with Cromwell\'s government from 1654, forcing Charles to turn for aid to Spain, which at that time ruled the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Netherlands" title="Southern Netherlands">Southern Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton198974\xe2\x80\x93112_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton198974\xe2\x80\x93112-18">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Charles made the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brussels_(1656)" title="Treaty of Brussels (1656)">Treaty of Brussels</a> with Spain in 1656. This gathered Spanish support for a restoration in return for Charles\'s contribution to the war against France. Charles raised a ragtag army from his exiled subjects; this small, underpaid, poorly-equipped and ill-disciplined force formed the nucleus of the post-Restoration army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979156\xe2\x80\x93157_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979156\xe2\x80\x93157-19">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The Commonwealth made the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1657)" title="Treaty of Paris (1657)">Treaty of Paris</a> with France in 1657 to join them in war against Spain in the Netherlands. Royalist supporters in the Spanish force were led by Charles\'s younger brother <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James, Duke of York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Dunes_(1658)" title="Battle of the Dunes (1658)">Battle of the Dunes</a> in 1658, as part of the larger Spanish force, Charles\'s army of around 2,000 clashed with Commonwealth troops fighting with the French. By the end of the battle Charles\'s force was about 1,000 and with Dunkirk given to the English the prospect of a Royalist expedition to England was dashed.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration">Restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(1660)" title="Restoration (1660)">Restoration (1660)</a></div>\n<p>After the death of Cromwell in 1658, Charles\'s chances of regaining the Crown at first seemed slim as Cromwell was succeeded as Lord Protector by his son, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cromwell" title="Richard Cromwell">Richard</a>. However, the new Lord Protector had little experience of either military or civil administration. In 1659, the <a href="/wiki/Rump_Parliament" title="Rump Parliament">Rump Parliament</a> was recalled and Richard resigned. During the civil and military unrest that followed, <a href="/wiki/George_Monck" class="mw-redirect" title="George Monck">George Monck</a>, the Governor of Scotland, was concerned that the nation would descend into anarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979160\xe2\x80\x93165_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979160\xe2\x80\x93165-22">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Monck and his army marched into the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a> and forced the Rump Parliament to re-admit members of the <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a> who had been excluded in December 1648 during <a href="/wiki/Pride%27s_Purge" title="Pride&#39;s Purge">Pride\'s Purge</a>. The Long Parliament dissolved itself and for the first time in almost 20 years, there was a general election.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The outgoing Parliament defined the electoral qualifications so as to ensure, as they thought, the return of a Presbyterian majority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199124\xe2\x80\x9325_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199124\xe2\x80\x9325-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The restrictions against royalist candidates and voters were widely ignored, and the elections resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">House of Commons</a> that was fairly evenly divided on political grounds between Royalists and Parliamentarians and on religious grounds between Anglicans and Presbyterians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199124\xe2\x80\x9325_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199124\xe2\x80\x9325-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The new so-called <a href="/wiki/Convention_Parliament_(1660)" title="Convention Parliament (1660)">Convention Parliament</a> assembled on 25 April 1660, and soon afterwards welcomed the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Breda" title="Declaration of Breda">Declaration of Breda</a>, in which Charles promised lenience and tolerance. There would be liberty of conscience and Anglican church policy would not be harsh. He would not exile past enemies nor confiscate their wealth. There would be pardons for nearly all his opponents except the <a href="/wiki/Regicides" class="mw-redirect" title="Regicides">regicides</a>. Above all, Charles promised to rule in cooperation with Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaley19855_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaley19855-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The English Parliament resolved to proclaim Charles king and invite him to return, a message that reached Charles at <a href="/wiki/Breda" title="Breda">Breda</a> on 8 May 1660.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989131_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989131-26">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In Ireland, a <a href="/wiki/Irish_Convention_(1660)" title="Irish Convention (1660)">convention</a> had been called earlier in the year, and had already declared for Charles. On 14 May, he was proclaimed king in Dublin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeaward2004_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeaward2004-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_arrival_of_King_Charles_II_of_England_in_Rotterdam,_may_24_1660_(Lieve_Pietersz._Verschuier,_1665).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Seascape of vessels along a low-lying coastline" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/The_arrival_of_King_Charles_II_of_England_in_Rotterdam%2C_may_24_1660_%28Lieve_Pietersz._Verschuier%2C_1665%29.jpg/220px-The_arrival_of_King_Charles_II_of_England_in_Rotterdam%2C_may_24_1660_%28Lieve_Pietersz._Verschuier%2C_1665%29.jpg" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/The_arrival_of_King_Charles_II_of_England_in_Rotterdam%2C_may_24_1660_%28Lieve_Pietersz._Verschuier%2C_1665%29.jpg/330px-The_arrival_of_King_Charles_II_of_England_in_Rotterdam%2C_may_24_1660_%28Lieve_Pietersz._Verschuier%2C_1665%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/The_arrival_of_King_Charles_II_of_England_in_Rotterdam%2C_may_24_1660_%28Lieve_Pietersz._Verschuier%2C_1665%29.jpg/440px-The_arrival_of_King_Charles_II_of_England_in_Rotterdam%2C_may_24_1660_%28Lieve_Pietersz._Verschuier%2C_1665%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1951" data-file-height="1037" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_arrival_of_King_Charles_II_of_England_in_Rotterdam,_may_24_1660_(Lieve_Pietersz._Verschuier,_1665).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles sailed from his exile in the Netherlands to his restoration in England in May 1660. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Lieve_Verschuier" title="Lieve Verschuier">Lieve Verschuier</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>He set out for England from <a href="/wiki/Scheveningen" title="Scheveningen">Scheveningen</a>, arrived in <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">Dover</a> on 25 May 1660 and reached London on 29 May, his 30th birthday. Although Charles and Parliament granted amnesty to nearly all of Cromwell\'s supporters in the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Indemnity_and_Oblivion" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Indemnity and Oblivion">Act of Indemnity and Oblivion</a>, 50 people were specifically excluded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979190_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979190-28">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In the end nine of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_regicides_of_Charles_I" title="List of regicides of Charles I">regicides</a> were executed:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Royal_Household2009_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Royal_Household2009-29">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> they were <a href="/wiki/Hanged,_drawn_and_quartered" title="Hanged, drawn and quartered">hanged, drawn and quartered</a>; others were given life imprisonment or simply excluded from office for life. The bodies of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ireton" title="Henry Ireton">Henry Ireton</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Bradshaw_(Judge)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Bradshaw (Judge)">John Bradshaw</a> were subjected to the indignity of <a href="/wiki/Posthumous_execution" title="Posthumous execution">posthumous decapitations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979185_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979185-30">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The English Parliament granted him an annual income to run the government of \xc2\xa31.2&#160;million,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalkus197294_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalkus197294-31">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> generated largely from customs and excise duties. The grant, however, proved to be insufficient for most of Charles\'s reign. For the most part, the actual revenue was much lower, which led to attempts to economise at court by reducing the size and expenses of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_household" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal household">royal household</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalkus197294_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalkus197294-31">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and raise money through unpopular innovations such as the <a href="/wiki/Chimney_money" class="mw-redirect" title="Chimney money">hearth tax</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeaward2004_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeaward2004-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the latter half of 1660, Charles\'s joy at the Restoration was tempered by the deaths of his youngest brother, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stuart,_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Henry Stuart, Duke of Gloucester">Henry</a>, and sister, <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Princess_Royal_and_Princess_of_Orange" title="Mary, Princess Royal and Princess of Orange">Mary</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>. At around the same time, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Hyde" title="Anne Hyde">Anne Hyde</a>, the daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hyde,_1st_Earl_of_Clarendon" title="Edward Hyde, 1st Earl of Clarendon">Edward Hyde</a>, revealed that she was pregnant by Charles\'s brother, <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James</a>, whom she had secretly married. Edward Hyde, who had not known of either the marriage or the pregnancy, was created <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Clarendon" title="Earl of Clarendon">Earl of Clarendon</a> and his position as Charles\'s favourite minister was strengthened.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clarendon_Code">Clarendon Code</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Clarendon Code">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Charles wearing a crown and ermine-lined robe" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg/220px-Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg/330px-Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg/440px-Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1268" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_II_by_John_Michael_Wright.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coronation portrait: Charles was crowned at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> on 23 April 1661.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The Convention Parliament was dissolved in December 1660, and, shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_monarch" title="Coronation of the British monarch">coronation</a>, the second English Parliament of the reign assembled. Dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Cavalier_Parliament" title="Cavalier Parliament">Cavalier Parliament</a>, it was overwhelmingly Royalist and Anglican. It sought to discourage <a href="/wiki/Non-conformist" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-conformist">non-conformity</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> and passed several acts to secure Anglican dominance. The <a href="/wiki/Corporation_Act_1661" title="Corporation Act 1661">Corporation Act 1661</a> required municipal officeholders to swear allegiance;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989169_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989169-34">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1662" title="Act of Uniformity 1662">Act of Uniformity 1662</a> made the use of the Anglican <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> compulsory; the <a href="/wiki/Conventicle_Act_1664" title="Conventicle Act 1664">Conventicle Act 1664</a> prohibited religious assemblies of more than five people, except under the auspices of the Church of England; and the <a href="/wiki/Five_Mile_Act_1665" title="Five Mile Act 1665">Five Mile Act 1665</a> prohibited expelled non-conforming clergymen from coming within five&#160;miles (8&#160;km) of a parish from which they had been banished. The Conventicle and Five Mile Acts remained in effect for the remainder of Charles\'s reign. The Acts became known as the <a href="/wiki/Clarendon_Code" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarendon Code">Clarendon Code</a>, after Lord Clarendon, even though he was not directly responsible for them and even spoke against the Five Mile Act.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989229_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989229-35">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Restoration was accompanied by social change. <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritanism</a> lost its momentum. Theatres reopened after having been closed during the <a href="/wiki/English_Interregnum" class="mw-redirect" title="English Interregnum">protectorship</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, and bawdy "<a href="/wiki/Restoration_comedy" title="Restoration comedy">Restoration comedy</a>" became a recognisable genre. Theatre licences granted by Charles required that female parts be played by "their natural performers", rather than by boys as was often the practice before;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989185_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989185-36">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Restoration_literature" title="Restoration literature">Restoration literature</a> celebrated or reacted to the restored court, which included <a href="/wiki/Libertine" title="Libertine">libertines</a> such as <a href="/wiki/John_Wilmot,_2nd_Earl_of_Rochester" title="John Wilmot, 2nd Earl of Rochester">John Wilmot, 2nd Earl of Rochester</a>. Of Charles II, Wilmot supposedly said:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>We have a pretty witty king,<br />\nWhose word no man relies on,<br />\nHe never said a foolish thing,<br />\nAnd never did a wise one"<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>To which Charles is reputed to have replied "that the matter was easily accounted for: For that his discourse was his own, his actions were the ministry\'s".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHume1778212_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHume1778212-38">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Plague_and_Great_Fire">Great Plague and Great Fire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Great Plague and Great Fire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1665, Charles was faced with a great health crisis: the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plague_of_London" title="Great Plague of London">Great Plague of London</a>. The death toll reached a peak of 7,000 per week in the week of 17 September.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979238_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979238-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Charles, with his family and court, fled London in July to <a href="/wiki/Salisbury" title="Salisbury">Salisbury</a>; Parliament met in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1991120_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1991120-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Plague cases ebbed over the winter, and Charles returned to London in February 1666.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalkus1972105_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalkus1972105-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a long spell of hot and dry weather through mid-1666, what later became known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_London" title="Great Fire of London">Great Fire of London</a> started on 2 September 1666 in a bakehouse on <a href="/wiki/Pudding_Lane" title="Pudding Lane">Pudding Lane</a>. Fanned by a strong easterly wind and fed by stockpiles of wood and fuel that had been prepared for the coming colder months, the fire eventually consumed about 13,200 houses and 87 churches, including <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul\'s Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter2007_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter2007-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Charles and his brother James joined and directed the fire-fighting effort. The public blamed Catholic conspirators for the fire,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and one Frenchman, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hubert" title="Robert Hubert">Robert Hubert</a>, was hanged on the basis of a false confession even though he had no hand in starting the fire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter2007_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter2007-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_and_colonial_policy">Foreign and colonial policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Foreign and colonial policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carlos_II_de_Inglaterra_e_Dona_Catarina_de_Bragan%C3%A7a_(gravura_holandesa,_3.%C2%BA_quartel_do_s%C3%A9culo_XVII).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Carlos_II_de_Inglaterra_e_Dona_Catarina_de_Bragan%C3%A7a_%28gravura_holandesa%2C_3.%C2%BA_quartel_do_s%C3%A9culo_XVII%29.png/220px-Carlos_II_de_Inglaterra_e_Dona_Catarina_de_Bragan%C3%A7a_%28gravura_holandesa%2C_3.%C2%BA_quartel_do_s%C3%A9culo_XVII%29.png" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Carlos_II_de_Inglaterra_e_Dona_Catarina_de_Bragan%C3%A7a_%28gravura_holandesa%2C_3.%C2%BA_quartel_do_s%C3%A9culo_XVII%29.png/330px-Carlos_II_de_Inglaterra_e_Dona_Catarina_de_Bragan%C3%A7a_%28gravura_holandesa%2C_3.%C2%BA_quartel_do_s%C3%A9culo_XVII%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Carlos_II_de_Inglaterra_e_Dona_Catarina_de_Bragan%C3%A7a_%28gravura_holandesa%2C_3.%C2%BA_quartel_do_s%C3%A9culo_XVII%29.png/440px-Carlos_II_de_Inglaterra_e_Dona_Catarina_de_Bragan%C3%A7a_%28gravura_holandesa%2C_3.%C2%BA_quartel_do_s%C3%A9culo_XVII%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1125" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carlos_II_de_Inglaterra_e_Dona_Catarina_de_Bragan%C3%A7a_(gravura_holandesa,_3.%C2%BA_quartel_do_s%C3%A9culo_XVII).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dutch engraving of Charles II and Catherine of Braganza</div></div></div>\n<p>Since 1640, Portugal had been fighting a <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Restoration_War" title="Portuguese Restoration War">war against Spain</a> to restore its independence after a <a href="/wiki/Dynastic_union" title="Dynastic union">dynastic union</a> of sixty years between the crowns of Spain and Portugal. Portugal had been helped by France, but in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_the_Pyrenees" title="Treaty of the Pyrenees">Treaty of the Pyrenees</a> in 1659 Portugal was abandoned by its French ally. Negotiations with Portugal for Charles\'s marriage to <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Braganza" title="Catherine of Braganza">Catherine of Braganza</a> began during his father\'s reign and upon the restoration, <a href="/wiki/Luisa_of_Medina-Sidonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Luisa of Medina-Sidonia">Queen Lu\xc3\xadsa of Portugal</a>, acting as regent, reopened negotiations with England that resulted in an alliance. On 23 June 1661, a marriage treaty was signed; England acquired Catherine\'s <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tangier" title="Tangier">Tangier</a> (in North Africa) and the <a href="/wiki/Seven_islands_of_Bombay" class="mw-redirect" title="Seven islands of Bombay">Seven islands of Bombay</a> (the latter having a major influence on the development of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in India), together with trading privileges in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a>, religious and commercial freedom in Portugal and two million Portuguese crowns (about \xc2\xa3300,000); while Portugal obtained military and naval support against Spain and liberty of worship for Catherine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne2004_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne2004-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Catherine journeyed from Portugal to <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a> on 13\xe2\x80\x9314 May 1662,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne2004_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne2004-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> but was not visited by Charles there until 20 May. The next day the couple were married at Portsmouth in two ceremonies\xe2\x80\x94a Catholic one conducted in secret, followed by a public <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne2004_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne2004-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The same year, in an unpopular move, Charles <a href="/wiki/Sale_of_Dunkirk" title="Sale of Dunkirk">sold Dunkirk</a> to his first cousin King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV of France</a> for about \xc2\xa3375,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199193,_99_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199193,_99-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The channel port, although a valuable strategic outpost, was a drain on Charles\'s limited finances.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CharlesII1667Medal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Obverse of medal" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/CharlesII1667Medal.jpg/220px-CharlesII1667Medal.jpg" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/CharlesII1667Medal.jpg/330px-CharlesII1667Medal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/CharlesII1667Medal.jpg/440px-CharlesII1667Medal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2050" data-file-height="1838" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CharlesII1667Medal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles II in profile on a medal struck in 1667 by <a href="/wiki/John_Roettier" class="mw-redirect" title="John Roettier">John Roettier</a> to commemorate the <a href="/wiki/Second_Dutch_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Dutch War">Second Dutch War</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Before Charles\'s restoration, the <a href="/wiki/Navigation_Acts" title="Navigation Acts">Navigation Acts</a> of 1650 had hurt <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch</a> trade by giving English vessels a monopoly, and had started the <a href="/wiki/First_Dutch_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First Dutch War">First Dutch War</a> (1652\xe2\x80\x931654). To lay foundations for a new beginning, envoys of the <a href="/wiki/States_General_of_the_Netherlands" title="States General of the Netherlands">States General</a> appeared in November 1660 with the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Gift" title="Dutch Gift">Dutch Gift</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsrael1998749\xe2\x80\x93750_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsrael1998749\xe2\x80\x93750-48">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Second_Dutch_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Dutch War">Second Dutch War</a> (1665\xe2\x80\x931667) was started by English attempts to muscle in on Dutch possessions in Africa and North America. The conflict began well for the English, with the capture of <a href="/wiki/New_Amsterdam" title="New Amsterdam">New Amsterdam</a> (renamed New York in honour of Charles\'s brother James, Duke of York) and a victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lowestoft" title="Battle of Lowestoft">Battle of Lowestoft</a>, but in 1667 the Dutch launched a surprise attack on England (the <a href="/wiki/Raid_on_the_Medway" title="Raid on the Medway">Raid on the Medway</a>) when they sailed up the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a> to where a major part of the English fleet was docked. Almost all of the ships were sunk except for the flagship, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Royal_Charles_(1655)" title="HMS Royal Charles (1655)"><i>Royal Charles</i></a>, which was taken back to the Netherlands as a trophy.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> The Second Dutch War ended with the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Breda_(1667)" title="Treaty of Breda (1667)">Treaty of Breda</a>.\n</p><p>As a result of the Second Dutch War, Charles dismissed <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hyde,_1st_Earl_of_Clarendon" title="Edward Hyde, 1st Earl of Clarendon">Lord Clarendon</a>, whom he used as a scapegoat for the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989250\xe2\x80\x93251_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989250\xe2\x80\x93251-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Clarendon fled to France when impeached for <a href="/wiki/High_treason" title="High treason">high treason</a> (which carried the penalty of death). Power passed to five politicians known collectively by a whimsical <a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym">acronym</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Cabal" title="Cabal">Cabal</a>\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Clifford,_1st_Baron_Clifford" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Clifford, 1st Baron Clifford">Clifford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bennet,_1st_Earl_of_Arlington" title="Henry Bennet, 1st Earl of Arlington">Arlington</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Villiers,_2nd_Duke_of_Buckingham" title="George Villiers, 2nd Duke of Buckingham">Buckingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley_Cooper,_1st_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley Cooper, 1st Earl of Shaftesbury">Ashley (afterwards Earl of Shaftesbury)</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Maitland,_1st_Duke_of_Lauderdale" title="John Maitland, 1st Duke of Lauderdale">Lauderdale</a>. In fact, the Cabal rarely acted in concert, and the court was often divided between two factions led by Arlington and Buckingham, with Arlington the more successful.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1668, England allied itself with Sweden, and with its former enemy the Netherlands, to oppose Louis XIV in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Devolution" title="War of Devolution">War of Devolution</a>. Louis made peace with the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Alliance_(1668)" title="Triple Alliance (1668)">Triple Alliance</a>, but he continued to maintain his aggressive intentions towards the Netherlands. In 1670, Charles, seeking to solve his financial troubles, agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Dover" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Dover">Treaty of Dover</a>, under which Louis XIV would pay him \xc2\xa3160,000 each year. In exchange, Charles agreed to supply Louis with troops and to announce his conversion to Catholicism "as soon as the welfare of his kingdom will permit".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979275_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979275-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Louis was to provide him with 6,000 troops to suppress those who opposed the conversion. Charles endeavoured to ensure that the Treaty\xe2\x80\x94especially the conversion clause\xe2\x80\x94remained secret.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> It remains unclear if Charles ever seriously intended to convert.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, by a series of five charters, Charles granted the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> the rights to autonomous government of its territorial acquisitions, to mint money, to command fortresses and troops, to form alliances, to make war and peace, and to exercise both civil and <a href="/wiki/Criminal_jurisdiction" title="Criminal jurisdiction">criminal jurisdiction</a> over its possessions in the Indies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911835_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911835-55">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Earlier in 1668 he leased the islands of <a href="/wiki/Bombay" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombay">Bombay</a> to the company for a nominal sum of \xc2\xa310 paid in gold.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBritish_Library_Learning_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBritish_Library_Learning-56">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Portuguese territories that Catherine brought with her as a dowry proved too expensive to maintain; <a href="/wiki/English_Tangier" title="English Tangier">Tangier</a> was abandoned in 1684.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989426_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989426-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 1670, Charles granted control of the entire <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> drainage basin to the <a href="/wiki/Hudson%27s_Bay_Company" title="Hudson&#39;s Bay Company">Hudson\'s Bay Company</a> by royal charter, and named the territory <a href="/wiki/Rupert%27s_Land" title="Rupert&#39;s Land">Rupert\'s Land</a>, after his cousin <a href="/wiki/Prince_Rupert_of_the_Rhine" title="Prince Rupert of the Rhine">Prince Rupert of the Rhine</a>, the company\'s first Governor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Hudson&#39;s_Bay_Company&#39;&#39;2017_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Hudson&#39;s_Bay_Company&#39;&#39;2017-58">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_with_Parliament">Conflict with Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Conflict with Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Although previously favourable to the Crown, the Cavalier Parliament was alienated by the king\'s wars and religious policies during the 1670s. In 1672, Charles issued the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Declaration_of_Indulgence" title="Royal Declaration of Indulgence">Royal Declaration of Indulgence</a>, in which he purported to suspend all <a href="/wiki/Penal_law_(Britain)" class="mw-redirect" title="Penal law (Britain)">penal laws</a> against Catholics and other religious dissenters. In the same year, he openly supported Catholic France and started the <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Dutch_War" title="Third Anglo-Dutch War">Third Anglo-Dutch War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Cavalier Parliament opposed the Declaration of Indulgence on constitutional grounds by claiming that the king had no right to arbitrarily suspend laws passed by Parliament. Charles withdrew the Declaration, and also agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Test_Act" title="Test Act">Test Act</a>, which not only required public officials to receive the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">sacrament</a> under the forms prescribed by the Church of England,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaithby1819782\xe2\x80\x93785_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaithby1819782\xe2\x80\x93785-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> but also later forced them to denounce certain teachings of the Catholic Church as "<a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstitious</a> and <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatrous</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaithby1819a894\xe2\x80\x93896_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaithby1819a894\xe2\x80\x93896-61">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Clifford, who had converted to Catholicism, resigned rather than take the oath, and committed suicide shortly after. By 1674 England had gained nothing from the Anglo-Dutch War, and the Cavalier Parliament refused to provide further funds, forcing Charles to make peace. The power of the Cabal waned and that of Clifford\'s replacement, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Osborne,_1st_Duke_of_Leeds" title="Thomas Osborne, 1st Duke of Leeds">Lord Danby</a>, grew.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles-pineapple.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Charles accepts a pineapple from a kneeling man in front of a grand country house" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Charles-pineapple.jpg/220px-Charles-pineapple.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Charles-pineapple.jpg/330px-Charles-pineapple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Charles-pineapple.jpg/440px-Charles-pineapple.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles-pineapple.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charles was presented with the first <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a> grown in England in 1675. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Hendrick_Danckerts" title="Hendrick Danckerts">Hendrick Danckerts</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Charles\'s wife Queen Catherine was unable to produce an heir; her four pregnancies had ended in <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillbirths</a> in 1662, February 1666, May 1668 and June 1669.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1996255\xe2\x80\x93257_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1996255\xe2\x80\x93257-5">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Charles\'s <a href="/wiki/Heir_presumptive" title="Heir presumptive">heir presumptive</a> was therefore his unpopular Catholic brother, James, Duke of York. Partly to assuage public fears that the royal family was too Catholic, Charles agreed that James\'s daughter, <a href="/wiki/Mary_II_of_England" title="Mary II of England">Mary</a>, should marry the Protestant <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William of Orange</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In 1678, <a href="/wiki/Titus_Oates" title="Titus Oates">Titus Oates</a>, who had been alternately an Anglican and <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> priest, falsely warned of a "<a href="/wiki/Popish_Plot" title="Popish Plot">Popish Plot</a>" to assassinate the king, even accusing the queen of complicity. Charles did not believe the allegations, but ordered his chief minister Lord Danby to investigate. While Danby seems to have been rightly sceptical about Oates\'s claims, the Cavalier Parliament took them seriously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989359\xe2\x80\x93362_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989359\xe2\x80\x93362-63">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The people were seized with an anti-Catholic hysteria;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979360_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979360-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> judges and juries across the land condemned the supposed conspirators; numerous innocent individuals were executed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979375_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979375-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later in 1678, Danby was impeached by the House of Commons on the charge of <a href="/wiki/High_treason" title="High treason">high treason</a>. Although much of the nation had sought war with Catholic France, Charles had secretly negotiated with <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a>, trying to reach an agreement under which England would remain neutral in return for money. Danby had publicly professed that he was hostile to France, but had reservedly agreed to abide by Charles\'s wishes. Unfortunately for him, the House of Commons failed to view him as a reluctant participant in the scandal, instead believing that he was the author of the policy. To save Danby from the impeachment trial, Charles dissolved the Cavalier Parliament in January 1679.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1991278,_301\xe2\x80\x93304_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1991278,_301\xe2\x80\x93304-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The new English Parliament, which met in March of the same year, was quite hostile to Charles. Many members feared that he had intended to use the standing army to suppress dissent or impose Catholicism. However, with insufficient funds voted by Parliament, Charles was forced to gradually disband his troops. Having lost the support of Parliament, Danby resigned his post of <a href="/wiki/Lord_High_Treasurer" title="Lord High Treasurer">Lord High Treasurer</a>, but received a pardon from the king. In defiance of the royal will, the House of Commons declared that the dissolution of Parliament did not interrupt impeachment proceedings, and that the pardon was therefore invalid. When the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> attempted to impose the punishment of exile\xe2\x80\x94which the Commons thought too mild\xe2\x80\x94the impeachment became stalled between the two Houses. As he had been required to do so many times during his reign, Charles bowed to the wishes of his opponents, committing Danby to the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>, in which he was held for another five years.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Later years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Charles faced a political storm over his brother James, a Catholic, being next in line to the throne. The prospect of a Catholic monarch was vehemently opposed by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley_Cooper,_1st_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley Cooper, 1st Earl of Shaftesbury">Anthony Ashley Cooper, 1st Earl of Shaftesbury</a> (previously Baron Ashley and a member of the Cabal, which had fallen apart in 1673). Shaftesbury\'s power base was strengthened when the House of Commons of 1679 introduced the <a href="/wiki/Exclusion_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Exclusion Bill">Exclusion Bill</a>, which sought to exclude the Duke of York from the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_British_throne" title="Succession to the British throne">line of succession</a>. Some even sought to confer the Crown on the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Monmouth" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Monmouth">Duke of Monmouth</a>, the eldest of Charles\'s illegitimate children. The <i>Abhorrers</i>\xe2\x80\x94those who thought the Exclusion Bill was abhorrent\xe2\x80\x94were named <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tories</a> (after a term for dispossessed Irish Catholic bandits), while the <i>Petitioners</i>\xe2\x80\x94those who supported a petitioning campaign in favour of the Exclusion Bill\xe2\x80\x94were called <a href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Whig Party">Whigs</a> (after a term for rebellious Scottish Presbyterians).<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_II_by_John_Riley.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Head and shoulders portrait of Charles with heavy jowls. He wears a wig of long black curls and a suit of armour." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/King_Charles_II_by_John_Riley.jpg/220px-King_Charles_II_by_John_Riley.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/King_Charles_II_by_John_Riley.jpg/330px-King_Charles_II_by_John_Riley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/King_Charles_II_by_John_Riley.jpg/440px-King_Charles_II_by_John_Riley.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2843" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Charles_II_by_John_Riley.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by <a href="/wiki/John_Riley_(painter)" title="John Riley (painter)">John Riley</a>, c. 1680\xe2\x80\x931685</div></div></div>\n<p>Fearing that the Exclusion Bill would be passed, and bolstered by some acquittals in the continuing Plot trials, which seemed to him to indicate a more favourable public mood towards Catholicism, Charles dissolved the English Parliament, for a second time that year, in mid-1679. Charles\'s hopes for a more moderate Parliament were not fulfilled; within a few months he had dissolved Parliament yet again, after it sought to pass the Exclusion Bill. When a new Parliament assembled at Oxford in March 1681, Charles dissolved it for a fourth time after just a few days.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> During the 1680s, however, popular support for the Exclusion Bill ebbed, and Charles experienced a nationwide surge of loyalty. Lord Shaftesbury was prosecuted (albeit unsuccessfully) for treason in 1681 and later fled to Holland, where he died. For the remainder of his reign, Charles ruled without Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989430\xe2\x80\x93441_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989430\xe2\x80\x93441-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Charles\'s opposition to the Exclusion Bill angered some Protestants. Protestant conspirators formulated the <a href="/wiki/Rye_House_Plot" title="Rye House Plot">Rye House Plot</a>, a plan to murder him and the Duke of York as they returned to London after horse races in <a href="/wiki/Newmarket,_Suffolk" title="Newmarket, Suffolk">Newmarket</a>. A great fire, however, destroyed Charles\'s lodgings at Newmarket, which forced him to leave the races early, thus inadvertently avoiding the planned attack. News of the failed plot was leaked.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979426_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979426-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Protestant politicians such as <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Capell,_1st_Earl_of_Essex" title="Arthur Capell, 1st Earl of Essex">Arthur Capell, 1st Earl of Essex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algernon_Sydney" class="mw-redirect" title="Algernon Sydney">Algernon Sydney</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Russell,_Lord_Russell" title="William Russell, Lord Russell">Lord William Russell</a> and the Duke of Monmouth were implicated in the plot. Lord Essex slit his own throat while imprisoned in the Tower of London; Sydney and Russell were executed for high treason on very flimsy evidence; and the Duke of Monmouth went into exile at the court of William of Orange. Lord Danby and the surviving Catholic lords held in the Tower were released and the king\'s Catholic brother, James, acquired greater influence at court.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Titus Oates was convicted and imprisoned for defamation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979437_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979437-73">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Charles suffered a sudden <a href="/wiki/Apoplexy" title="Apoplexy">apoplectic fit</a> on the morning of 2 February 1685, and died aged 54 at 11:45&#160;am four days later at <a href="/wiki/Whitehall_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitehall Palace">Whitehall Palace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The suddenness of his illness and death led to suspicion of poison in the minds of many, including one of the royal doctors; however, a more modern medical analysis has held that the symptoms of his final illness are similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Uremia" title="Uremia">uraemia</a> (a clinical syndrome due to kidney dysfunction).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBMJ1938_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBMJ1938-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In the days between his collapse and his death, Charles endured a variety of torturous treatments including <a href="/wiki/Bloodletting" title="Bloodletting">bloodletting</a>, purging and <a href="/wiki/Cupping_therapy" title="Cupping therapy">cupping</a> in hopes of effecting a recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts2015_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts2015-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On his deathbed Charles asked his brother, James, to look after his mistresses: "be well to <a href="/wiki/Louise_de_K%C3%A9rouaille,_Duchess_of_Portsmouth" title="Louise de K\xc3\xa9rouaille, Duchess of Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a>, and let not poor <a href="/wiki/Nell_Gwyn" title="Nell Gwyn">Nelly</a> starve".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979456_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979456-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> He told his courtiers, "I am sorry, gentlemen, for being such a time a-dying",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryant200173_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryant200173-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and expressed regret at his treatment of his wife. On the last evening of his life he was received into the Catholic Church, though the extent to which he was fully conscious or committed, and with whom the idea originated, is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989443,_456_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989443,_456-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> He was buried in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> "without any manner of pomp"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryant200173_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryant200173-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> on 14 February.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979459_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979459-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Charles was succeeded by his brother, who became <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James II of England</a> and Ireland and James VII of Scotland.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_II_statue._Parliament_Square_Edinburgh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Lead equestrian statue" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Charles_II_statue._Parliament_Square_Edinburgh.JPG/170px-Charles_II_statue._Parliament_Square_Edinburgh.JPG" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Charles_II_statue._Parliament_Square_Edinburgh.JPG/255px-Charles_II_statue._Parliament_Square_Edinburgh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Charles_II_statue._Parliament_Square_Edinburgh.JPG/340px-Charles_II_statue._Parliament_Square_Edinburgh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1809" data-file-height="2273" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_II_statue._Parliament_Square_Edinburgh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Charles II as a Roman Caesar, erected 1685, <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Square,_Edinburgh" title="Parliament Square, Edinburgh">Parliament Square, Edinburgh</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Biographer <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Hutton" title="Ronald Hutton">Ronald Hutton</a> says Charles was a popular king in his own day and a "legendary figure" in British history.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Other kings had inspired more respect, but perhaps only Henry VIII had endeared himself to the popular imagination as much as this one. He was the playboy monarch, naughty but nice, the hero of all who prized urbanity, tolerance, good humour, and the pursuit of pleasure above the more earnest, sober, or material virtues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutton1989446_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutton1989446-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Biographer <a href="/wiki/Hilaire_Belloc" title="Hilaire Belloc">Hilaire Belloc</a> states:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Charles was universally beloved, beloved not only by the crowd of individuals with whom he came in contact, not only adored by his dependents, but thoroughly popular with the mass of his subjects and particularly with the poorer populace of London who knew him best.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The escapades of Charles after his defeat at the Battle of Worcester remained important to him throughout his life. He delighted and bored listeners with tales of his escape for many years. Numerous accounts of his adventures were published, particularly in the immediate aftermath of the Restoration. Though not averse to his escape being ascribed to divine providence, Charles himself seems to have delighted most in his ability to sustain his disguise as a man of ordinary origins, and to move unrecognised through his realm. Ironic and cynical, Charles took pleasure in retailing stories which demonstrated the undetectable nature of any inherent majesty he possessed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeber1988492-493,_505-506_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeber1988492-493,_505-506-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Charles had no legitimate children, but acknowledged a dozen by seven mistresses,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser1979411_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser1979411-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> including five by <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Palmer,_1st_Duchess_of_Cleveland" title="Barbara Palmer, 1st Duchess of Cleveland">Barbara Villiers, Lady Castlemaine</a>, for whom the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Cleveland" title="Duke of Cleveland">Dukedom of Cleveland</a> was created.
